    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90297294acb54d65bda1fd4c7abc9bfe">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '84c1ce7c-a5e5-4285-bc1e-d30b65e1f8b7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d10724113" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries are emerging as promising candidates for grid energy storage because of the abundant sodium resources and low cost. However, the identification and development of suitable anode materials is far from being satisfactory. Here, it is demonstrated that the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets with tunnel structure can be used as suitable anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets are synthesized by liquid exfoliation combined with topotactic dehydration, delivering a high reversible capacity of 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> at a suitable average voltage of ≈0.7 V. It is found that a low energy diffusion barrier, enlarged interlayer spacing, and exceptional nanoporosity together give rise to high rate performance characterized by pseudocapacitive behavior. The observed high reversible capacity, excellent rate capability, and good cyclability of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets make this material competitive when compared to other sodium insertion anode materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201804378-sec-0010">
         
         <p>Sodium ions batteries (SIBs) are appealing alternatives to lithium ions batteries (LIBs) systems for large-scale energy storage applications because of the high natural abundance of elemental sodium.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0001_R_d10723946e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>, <a href="#adma201804378-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0002_R_d10723946e522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Rev.">2</a></span> The Na<sup>+</sup> ions have larger ionic radius and larger molar mass than Li<sup>+</sup> ions, leading to sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics and to large volume variation during Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion and extraction. Thus, most SIBs electrode materials have rather low specific capacity and poor capacity retention upon cycling when compared to the state-of-the-art LIBs.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0003_R_d10723946e533" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Energy">3</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Plenty of cathodes for SIBs have been studied, while suitable anode materials are quite limited. Contrary to Li, it has been experimentally and theoretically evidenced that Na hardly intercalates into graphite due to the limited interlayer distance.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0004_R_d10723946e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Solid State Ionics">4</a>, <a href="#adma201804378-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0005_R_d10723946e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Power Sources">5</a></span> Hard carbon materials with low graphitization and larger interlayer distances are electrochemically active in SIBs;<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0006_R_d10723946e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun.">6</a></span> however, the cycle stability was found insufficient and safety issues such as sodium dendrites may be caused by the low reaction voltage range (below 0.1 V). Materials relying on alloying/dealloying and conversion reactions exhibit high initial capacity, but suffer from severe capacity degradation most likely due to the large volume variation and the sluggish kinetics.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0007_R_d10723946e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Commun.">7</a>-<a href="#adma201804378-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0009_R_d10723946e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></span> Searching for noncarbonaceous Na-insertion host anodes with fast solid state kinetics and long term stability is urgently needed to overcome the intrinsic limitations of SIBs. Recently, several different types of titanium/vanadium/molybdenum-based oxides have been proposed as anode materials for SIBs, but the specific capacities of these compounds are limited.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0010_R_d10723946e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Electrochem. Commun.">10</a>-<a href="#adma201804378-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0014_R_d10723946e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></span> For example, Passerini et al. reported that anatase shows an irreversible reaction and that the newly formed amorphous sodium titanate phase is able to accommodate ≈0.41 sodium per TiO<sub>2</sub>, providing a reversible specific capacity of only 140 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0013_R_d10723946e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a></span> Layered Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> was reported as an anode for SIBs, which accommodates two Na ions per formula at a low reaction voltage ≈0.30 V versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, corresponding to ≈178 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0015_R_d10723946e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">15</a></span> Nevertheless, it suffers from a low initial Coulombic efficiency of &lt;50% and capacity decay upon cycling.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0016_R_d10723946e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Mater.">16</a></span> Ternary metal oxides with two different metal cations are expected to deliver high specific capacities by using multiple redox couples.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0017_R_d10723946e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">17</a>-<a href="#adma201804378-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0019_R_d10723946e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Nat. Commun.">19</a></span> Mixed titanium–niobium oxides, such as TiNb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0020_R_d10723946e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Chem. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#adma201804378-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0021_R_d10723946e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nanoscale">21</a></span> have been studied as electrode materials for LIBs exhibiting improved lithium storage performances. Identification and development of suitable ternary metal oxide anodes which combine high specific Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity with high rate capability is of key importance.</p>
         
         <p>Here, we report an excellent sodium-storage performance in Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets, synthesized by liquid exfoliation combined with thermal dehydration. A material with the empty tunnel structure and exceptional nanoporosity is formed during the topotactic dehydration of ultrathin HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> sheets. The Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets exhibit an average storage voltage of ≈0.7 V characterized by an efficient four sodium storage per formula unit with a stable reversible capacity of 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The ultrathin nanosheets combined with an abundant nanovoid architecture provide near-surface redox sites reaching several nm into the material and resulting in a pseudocapacitively dominated charge storage process in Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets as revealed by kinetic analysis; this mechanism is favored at high charge/discharge rates and gives excellent capacities at these rates. By utilizing multiple redox couples and tailoring the microstructure, the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets demonstrate an improved performance in terms of high reversible capacity, rapid charge and discharge, as well as excellent cycle life and stability.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The ultrathin Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets were obtained by exfoliation of protonated cation-exchangeable layered metal oxides followed by a topotactic dehydration of the HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> nanosheets. The schematics shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0001">1</a>a illustrate the formation steps of the ultrathin Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets. First, layered KTiNbO<sub>5</sub> crystals were synthesized by a classical solid-state reaction between stoichiometric amounts of K<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, TiO<sub>2</sub>, and Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. The protonated form HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> was obtained via proton exchange reaction of KTiNbO<sub>5</sub> with HNO<sub>3</sub> aqueous solution. Tetrabutylammonium hydroxide (TBAOH) was chosen to introduce bulky organic ions via exchange with the protons. The insertion of voluminous and hydrophilic TBA<sup>+</sup> cations expands the interlayer spacing, resulting in the exfoliation of individual metal oxide sheets. Finally, an annealing treatment at 400 °C is used to topotactically convert the HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> nanosheets into a tunnel structure, Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub>, as described by<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0022_R_d10723946e665" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Mater. Res. Bull.">22</a></span><div class="inline-equation" id="adma201804378-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE--><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/01284329-8739-4264-8ff1-1e6c62a6248f/adma201804378-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201804378:adma201804378-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adma201804378-math-0001.png">
                     <mrow>
                        <mn>2</mn>
                        <msub>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">HTiNbO</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <mn>5</mn>
                        </msub>
                        <mo>→</mo>
                        <msub>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">Ti</mi>
                           </mrow>

                        </msub>

                           </mrow>

                        </msub>
                        <mo>+</mo>
                        <msub>

                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0a4d0192-1d90-488d-bc70-e1b8f29ec8b3/adma201804378-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7790b282-e35b-4e2a-8d1f-339369e48312/adma201804378-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0a4d0192-1d90-488d-bc70-e1b8f29ec8b3/adma201804378-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201804378-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201804378" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Synthesis of ultrathin metal oxides nanosheets. a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis process of ultrathin Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets. b) XRD patterns of bulk KTiNbO<sub>5</sub>, bulk HTiNbO<sub>5</sub>, and HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> nanosheets. c) SEM image of KTiNbO<sub>5</sub> particle. d) TEM image of ultrathin HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> nanosheets. e) AFM image and f) its corresponding thickness analysis taken along the white lines in (e). g) Photograph shows the dispersion of bulk KTiNbO<sub>5</sub>, bulk HTiNbO<sub>5</sub>, and HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> nanosheets in water.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Powder X-ray diffraction (XRD), scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) were used to study the effect of proton exchange and chemical exfoliation on the microstructure of the samples. As seen from Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0001">1</a>b, all the diffraction peaks of the KTiNbO<sub>5</sub> specimen could be indexed to a layered structure with an orthorhombic crystal KTiNbO<sub>5</sub> phase (JCPDS 72-1076). The KTiNbO<sub>5</sub> samples were composed of tabular particles of up to several micrometers (Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0001">1</a>c and Figure S1, Supporting Information). A <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.94 nm was deduced corresponding to the (002) plane based on the Bragg equation λ = 2<i>d</i>sinθ. KTiNbO<sub>5</sub> has a lamellar structure built up of alternating 2 × 2 (Ti/Nb)O<sub>6</sub> edge-sharing octahedral and two sets of interlayer K<sup>+</sup> ions. There are two dominant planes, (002) plane and (200) plane, on the basis of XRD diffraction peaks. As shown in Supplementary Figure S2c,d (Supporting Information), the (200) plane exhibits a unique open frame with a large <i>d</i>-spacing of ≈0.94 nm that is consistent with XRD result, according to which the positively charged K<sup>+</sup> ions occupy the interlayer space. Large voids are obtained when potassium cations are eliminated, which can serve as host to accommodate Na<sup>+</sup> ion without a significant strain on the crystal structure. Different from the (002) plane, the (200) plane consists of infinite blocks of (Ti/Nb)O<sub>6</sub> layers with a small <i>d</i>-spacing of ≈0.32 nm (Figure S2e,f, Supporting Information). It may be speculated that Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion in the (002) plane is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing. After acid exchange, the (002) reflection was shifted toward a higher 2θ angle (10.4°) with a decrease of <i>d</i>-spacing of ≈0.85 nm, attributed to the replacement of K<sup>+</sup> by the small H<sup>+</sup> ions in the interlayer space (Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0001">1</a>b). No significant changes in morphology due to proton exchange were observed in the HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> bulk (Figure S3a,b, Supporting Information). The XRD and TEM studies demonstrate that the interlayer spacing in the layered titanoniobates can be adjusted by changing the cations. The protons are then exchanged by bulky organic ions (tetrabutylammonium cations), leading to substantial swelling and finally chemical exfoliation of HTiNbO<sub>5</sub>. The TEM image of the exfoliated products depicted in Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0001">1</a>d clearly shows a large-area 2D sheet-like morphology with transparent appearance, indicating their ultrathin nature. The morphology and thickness of the exfoliated sheets is further characterized by atomic force microscopy (AFM). Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0001">1</a>e shows a typical tapping mode AFM image of exfoliated nanosheets deposited on a mica substrate by spin-coating. The lateral dimensions of the isolated nanosheets are about 1–3 µm. The corresponding height profile in Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0001">1</a>f shows that the average topographic height is around 1.2–1.3 nm, which is close to the estimated value (1.05 nm) from the ionic radii of its components.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0023_R_d10723946e806" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem.">23</a></span> The slightly larger values measured from AFM observations are likely due to surface adsorption of solvents or guest species on nanosheets. This demonstrates that the exfoliated HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> is likely to consist of unilamellar nanosheets. As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0001">1</a>b, the exfoliated HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> nanosheets displays a much weaker (002) diffraction peak intensity. Meanwhile, the characteristic (002) diffraction peak was shifted to a lower 2θ angle, suggesting that the HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> nanosheets have a large interlayer spacing of 12.3 Å. Different from the bulk materials, the colloidal dispersions of the exfoliated products are stable over one month, indicating the production of homogeneous 2D ultrathin sheets (Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0001">1</a>g).</p>
         
         <p>The Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets can be easily obtained from topotactic dehydration of the HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> nanosheets. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0002">2</a>a, Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets displayed weak and broad diffraction peaks. The structural resolution from these data remains impossible but shows the characteristic diffraction peaks similar to previous reports.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0024_R_d10723946e842" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Mater.">24</a></span> The chemical composition and valence band structures of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> were further analyzed by using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and the results shown in Figure S4 (Supporting Information). The high-resolution Ti 2p XPS spectrum presents two peaks centered at 458.6 and 464.3 eV, corresponding respectively to the Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub> photoelectrons in the Ti<sup>4+</sup> state.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0025_R_d10723946e857" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Mater.">25</a></span> For Nb 3d XPS spectrum, two peaks were observed at binding energies of 207.1 and 209.8 eV representing Nb<sup>5+</sup> 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0026_R_d10723946e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Mater.">26</a></span> The O 1s peak appeared at 530.1 eV is attributed to the presence of Nb<span class="icomoon"></span>O and Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds (Figure S4c, Supporting Information). SEM images of the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets in Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0002">2</a>b reveal a well-interconnected porous framework of randomly oriented, crinkly sheets, similar to those of previous graphene aerogels. The high magnification SEM image clearly demonstrates the free-standing nature of the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets (Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0002">2</a>c). These curls and wrinkles formed in the freeze-drying process effectively prevent the restacking of oxide nanosheets sheets during annealing, leading to higher porosity and larger specific surface area which enhance the electrochemical performances of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub>. In contrast, the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> bulk consists of particles with sizes of 0.2–3 µm, each consisting of tightly stacked layers (Figure S5, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure S6a (Supporting Information), the ultrathin nature of nanosheets can be deduced from the nearly transparent feature of the nanosheets. The presences of abundant defects and/or nanovoids, indicated by a red circle, are clearly seen in the annular dark-field scanning TEM image (Figure S6b, Supporting Information) and high resolution (HR)TEM image (Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0002">2</a>d). The local formation of these “holes” of few nanometers could be attributed to a loss of matter in the course of the topotactic reaction. The few-layers structure can be clearly observed in HRTEM images at the occasionally found folded edges (Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0002">2</a>e). One nanosheet has four layers and the other contains two. The interlayer spacing of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets is about 0.85 nm, which is larger than that of bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> (≈0.75 nm, Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0002">2</a>f), possibly due to reduced interlayer binding energy. As we will show below Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets hold great potential for fast sodium storage because of their intriguing properties including fast electrochemical reaction, ultrahigh surface area, expanded interlayer spacing and abundant nanoporosity.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201804378-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0b778a54-75b3-4c96-8031-65937c7d60ca/adma201804378-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0b778a54-75b3-4c96-8031-65937c7d60ca/adma201804378-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/52e08f66-7ebd-4584-af28-0891e1cc8a02/adma201804378-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0b778a54-75b3-4c96-8031-65937c7d60ca/adma201804378-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201804378-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201804378" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Microstructural of ultrathin Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets and bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub>. a) XRD pattern of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets, b,c) SEM images, and d) HRTEM image of ultrathin Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets. Cross-sectional TEM images of e) ultrathin Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets and f) bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub>. Intensity profiles along the arrows indicate interlayer spacings of corresponding samples.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Coin-type cell configuration was used to evaluate the electrochemical properties of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets as an anode. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0003">3</a>a displays the galvanostatic discharge−charge voltage curves of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheet electrode delivers discharge and charge capacities of 289 and 237 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, with a Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 82% in the first cycle. This CE value is higher than other titanium/niobium based metal oxides electrodes that usually suffer from low CE of ≈50%.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0027_R_d10723946e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem. Commun.">27</a>-<a href="#adma201804378-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0030_R_d10723946e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Energy">30</a></span> Noticeably, the discharge−charge profiles of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> are completely sloping, it appears that a solid-solution type process is associated with Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation. This characteristic might be confirmed by the XRD pattern of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> electrode at the end of the first discharge and charge, shown in Figure S7 (Supporting Information). The other interesting benefit of the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> anode is, though energetically less attractive, the safer average storage voltage of ≈0.7 V, when, e.g., compared to layered Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> (0.3 V) which is closer to the sodium plating voltage.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0031_R_d10723946e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Commun.">31</a></span> The rate performance of the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets electrode is shown in Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0003">3</a>b,c. Increasing the current rate has a very small effect on the discharge/charge voltage curves (Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0003">3</a>b). If increased from 100 to 200, 500, 1000, and 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the reversible capacity slightly decreased from 242 to 229, 208, 182, and 157 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even at the high current density of 4000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, significantly high discharge capacity of 134 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (55% of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> capacity) is reached. The results demonstrated that the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets electrode exhibits excellent power capability, which is better than other Ti-based insertion anodes (Table S1, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheet electrode delivers an outstanding capacity retention of 86% even after 500 cycles with a high Coulombic efficiency (CE) of &gt;99% at a current density of 800 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0003">3</a>d). The capacity loss might be ascribed to side reactions occurring between electrode materials and electrolyte during cycling.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0032_R_d10723946e1082" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Sci. Adv.">32</a></span> The electrochemical performance of H<i><sub>x</sub></i>TiNbO<sub>5</sub> calcined at 300 °C and bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> electrode were also evaluated. Although H<i><sub>x</sub></i>TiNbO<sub>5</sub> exhibits a high initial discharge capacity of 430 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> but suffers from serious capacity decay (Figure S8, Supporting Information). The inferior cycle performance of H<i><sub>x</sub></i>TiNbO<sub>5</sub> resulting from the side reaction with the electrolyte and repulsion force between H<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> ions.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0008_R_d10723946e1114" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Sci. Adv.">8</a></span> The bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> electrode exhibits a considerably lower capacity and inferior power performance compared with Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheet electrode (Figure S9, Supporting Information). For instance, at a high current density of 4000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets electrode was nearly four times higher than bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> (134 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> vs 36 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>). The outstanding sodium storage properties of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets can be ascribed to the following features of the design: i) The ultrathin nanosheets of several nanometers significantly shorten the transport distance for both lithium ions and electrons; ii) The slightly enlarged interlayer spacing in Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets allows for fast sodium ions diffusion due to reduced sodium diffusion barriers.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201804378-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2e328d85-343e-4aee-823e-3ab22b715a8e/adma201804378-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2e328d85-343e-4aee-823e-3ab22b715a8e/adma201804378-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/914692e4-f708-4df1-a2ba-c40de3615dc5/adma201804378-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2e328d85-343e-4aee-823e-3ab22b715a8e/adma201804378-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201804378-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201804378" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium storage performance of the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets electrode. a) Discharge–charge voltage profiles at rate of 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Discharge–charge voltage profiles at various current densities from 100 to 4000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate performance at various current densities from 100 to 4000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Cycle retention and the corresponding Coulombic efficiency for 500 cycles at a current density of 800 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">To better understand the differences between Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets and bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub>, sweep-rate-dependent cyclic voltammetry (CV) experiments were performed to investigate their kinetics and charge storage mechanism. The CV curves of the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets electrode at different scan rates is presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0004">4</a>a. The broad cathodic and anodic peaks correspond well to the galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles. With a 100-fold increment in scan rate from 0.1 to 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the broad peaks were retained and not significantly altered in all CV curves. In contrast, there are peak shift and change in shape of the oxidation/reduction potential peaks as the sweep rate increases in bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> electrode (Figure S10, Supporting Information). In simple cases, the current (<i>i</i>) and sweep rates (<i>v</i>) obey the following power law<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0033_R_d10723946e1233" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Phys. Chem. B">33</a></span><div class="inline-equation" id="adma201804378-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE--><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/561421d0-70af-4dce-9cc6-f62c3ab90b51/adma201804378-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201804378:adma201804378-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/adma201804378-math-0002.png">
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>i</mi>
                        <mo> </mo>
                        <mo>=</mo>
                        <mo> </mo>
                        <mi>a</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                           <mi>b</mi>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable values. In particular, the <i>b</i>-value of 0.5 indicates a totally diffusion-controlled process, whereas 1.0 indicates a capacitive process, which can be determined by the slope of the log(<i>v</i>) against log(<i>i</i>) plots. As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0004">4</a>b, the <i>b</i> value for the bulk electrode is 0.56, which reveals a diffusion-controlled mechanism. While the <i>b</i> values for Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets electrode is 0.85, demonstrating that the majority charge storage process exhibits capacitive characteristics. The total capacitive contribution to the current response could be further quantitatively determined by separating the current response (<i>i</i>) at a fixed potential (<i>V</i>) into capacitive effects (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) and diffusion-controlled processes (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>) according to<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0034_R_d10723946e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nat. Mater.">34</a></span><div class="inline-equation" id="adma201804378-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE--><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/884751ae-c76f-429a-86b0-9e45ca2923e0/adma201804378-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:adma201804378:adma201804378-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/adma201804378-math-0003.png">
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>i</mi>
                        <mo> </mo>
                        <mrow>
                           <mfenced>
                              <mi>V</mi>
                           </mfenced>
                        </mrow>
                        <mo>=</mo>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>k</mi>
                           <mn>1</mn>
                        </msub>
                        <mo> </mo>
                        <mi>v</mi>
                        <mo>+</mo>
                        <msub>
                           <mi>k</mi>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                        </msub>
                        <msup>
                           <mi>v</mi>
                           <mrow>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                              <mo>/</mo>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </mrow>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are constants at a particular sweep rate. We have used this methodology to determine the fraction of the total stored charge from capacitive processes. For Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheet electrode, around 59% of the total current (and therefore, the capacity) is capacitive at 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, which is denoted by the red shaded region in Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0004">4</a>c. In addition, the capacitive contribution gradually improves with increase of scan rate, and achieves a maximum value of 91.3% at 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0004">4</a>d). A comparison of the fraction of the total stored charge from capacitive processes as a function of sweep rate for Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> NS and bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> is shown in Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0004">4</a>e. The capacitive contribution for bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> is significantly lower than that for the nanosheets ones. Even at high scan rate, the capacitive contribution is as high as 91.3% in the case of nanosheets while only 11% for bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub>.</div>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201804378-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b3ee34e8-a337-40a9-ad38-62a629b3d54c/adma201804378-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b3ee34e8-a337-40a9-ad38-62a629b3d54c/adma201804378-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4e694824-edc0-4a0c-a9c9-11f253ef5bfd/adma201804378-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b3ee34e8-a337-40a9-ad38-62a629b3d54c/adma201804378-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201804378-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201804378" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Kinetic analysis of the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets electrode. a) CV curves at various scan rates from 0.1 to 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Determination of the <i>b</i> value using the relationship between peak current and scan rate. c) Separation of the capacitive and diffusion currents at a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. d) Contribution ratio of the capacitive and diffusion-controlled charge versus scan rate. e) The comparison of capacitive contribution. f) Capacity versus <i>v</i><sup>−1/2</sup> allows for the separation of diffusion-controlled capacity from capacitive-controlled capacity.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The nature of the charge storage mechanism can also be further confirmed from the relationship between capacity and sweep rate.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0035_R_d10723946e1396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Electrochim. Acta">35</a>, <a href="#adma201804378-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0036_R_d10723946e1396" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Electrochim. Acta">36</a></span> Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0004">4</a>f shows the plot of normalized capacity (<i>Q</i>) versus <i>v</i><sup>−1/2</sup> for Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets and bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub>, respectively. For bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub>, the capacity decreases rather linearly with <i>v</i><sup>−1/2</sup> indicating that charge storage is mainly controlled by sodium diffusion. However, the capacity for Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets does not vary so much with the increase of scan rate. As discussed above, the charge storage mechanism for Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets is vastly different from that of bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub>. Specifically, the latter exhibits a diffusion-controlled mechanism in contrast to the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets.</p>
         
         <p>The fast kinetics of the pseudocapacitive storage process in Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets might be largely attributed to its intriguing microstructure/architecture. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed to gain insight into the sodium dynamics process in Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub>. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0005">5</a>a, there are large voids surrounded by octahedra around Nb or Ti, which can serve as hosts for sodium ions. The voids can stably maintain the structure even when sodium ions are adsorbed. The preferential orientation of 1D channels in the [100] direction provides the shortest transporting pathway for sodium ions. The lowest diffusion barrier of Na atoms in Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> is calculated to be ≈0.20 eV along the [100] direction (Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0005">5</a>b), which is much smaller than that for Na diffusion in other Ti-based oxides, such as Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> (≈0.91 eV),<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0009_R_d10723946e1490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></span> Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Li<sub>0.22</sub>Ti<sub>0.78</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (≈0.4 eV).<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0031_R_d10723946e1501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Commun.">31</a></span> The few-layered Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> combined with an abundant nanovoid architecture provides a multitude of redox sites very close to the surface and hence very short transport distances for both sodium ions and electrons enabling fast intercalation kinetics. Moreover, the 2D ultrathin nanosheets structure with high surface area could provide many available extra sites for sodium accommodation at interfaces (interfacial storage), similar to that of 2D surface adsorption reactions.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201804378-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ddfd9b71-b0cf-4662-8afd-112f2f2fcfd8/adma201804378-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ddfd9b71-b0cf-4662-8afd-112f2f2fcfd8/adma201804378-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0872710f-008e-4137-8f5c-1aa87a6fbe80/adma201804378-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ddfd9b71-b0cf-4662-8afd-112f2f2fcfd8/adma201804378-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201804378-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201804378" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">DFT calculations. a) Schematic illustration of the unit cell structure of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> projected along <i>a</i> axis. b) The migration barrier of Na ions through the 1D channel along with [100] direction. c) The comparison of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheet electrode with other insertion based SIBs anodes in terms of average voltage and reversible capacity.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Figure <a href="#adma201804378-fig-0005">5</a>c summarizes insertion-based SIBs anodes that have recently been reported, in which major electrochemical parameters, such as average voltages and reversible capacity, are included. Most intercalation anode materials suffer from low specific capacity (below 200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) due to the limited intercalation sites available for Na ions in the host lattice. On the contrary, ternary Ti−Nb-based oxides contain multiple redox couples of Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup>, Nb<sup>5+</sup>/Nb<sup>4+</sup>. The theoretical capacity of Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> is 252 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> according to the 4 electron transfer reaction. The Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets electrode delivers a high reversible capacity of 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> along with reasonable average voltages, which surpasses carbonaceous materials (graphite,<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0037_R_d10723946e1574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">37</a></span> hard carbon<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0038_R_d10723946e1576" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">38</a></span>), titanium/niobium/stannum based oxides (TiO<sub>2</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0039_R_d10723946e1581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Power Sources">39</a></span> Li<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0009_R_d10723946e1589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Commun.">9</a></span> Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0030_R_d10723946e1596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Energy">30</a></span> NaVSnO<sub>4</sub><span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0014_R_d10723946e1599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a></span>), layered sodium metal oxides (Na<sub>0.6</sub>[Cr<sub>0.6</sub>Ti<sub>0.4</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0040_R_d10723946e1610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nat. Commun.">40</a></span> Na<sub>0.66</sub>[Li<sub>0.22</sub>Ti<sub>0.78</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0031_R_d10723946e1621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Commun.">31</a></span> NaTiO<sub>2</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0041_R_d10723946e1625" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Energy Environ. Sci.">41</a></span> Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub><span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0016_R_d10723946e1633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Mater.">16</a></span>) and phosphates (NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub><span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0042_R_d10723946e1640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">42</a></span>). Overall, the above-emphasized features reveal that the Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> anode has great potential for next generation SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In summary, we have successfully prepared ultrathin Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets in high-yield through a scalable liquid exfoliation strategy followed by topotactic dehydration. The obtained Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets exhibit an abundant nanovoid architecture throughout the whole nanosheets, and enlarged interlayer spacings when compared with bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub>. Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets deliver an impressive reversible capacity of 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, excellent rate capability and an average voltage of ≈0.7 V compromising the energy density but making the battery safer. DFT calculation found that the lowest diffusion barrier of Na atoms in Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> is ≈0.20 eV along the [100] direction. A pseudocapacitive storage process in Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets was revealed by a kinetic analysis, which not only enhances the overall storage capacity but also enables very fast charge/discharge rates. The achieved excellent electrochemical performance in Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> sheets is better than for existing sodium insertion anode materials. Our design strategy, i.e., utilizing multiple redox couples and tailoring microstructure, may inspire the development of a wide range of high-performance electrode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201804378-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma201804378-sec-0020-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Materials Synthesis</i>: The layered KTiNbO<sub>5</sub> was prepared by heating a stoichiometric mixture of K<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, TiO<sub>2</sub>, and Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> at 1050 °C for 10 h. The protonated form HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> was obtained via the proton exchange reaction of KTiNbO<sub>5</sub> with 6 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> HNO<sub>3</sub> aqueous solution for two days. After performing the above process three times, the solid product was recovered by centrifugation, washed thoroughly with DI water to remove excess acid and then dried in air. To prepare HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> nanosheets, 1.0 g HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> was added to 100 mL DI water. Tetrabutylammonium hydroxide (TBA<sup>+</sup>OH<sup>−</sup>) solution was slowly added to the above suspension until the pH reached 9.5–10.0, and the resultant solution was shaken for 7 days. After the exfoliation reaction, the incompletely exfoliated particles were removed by centrifugation, and the supernatant solution containing the nanosheets was collected. The dilute HCl solution was used to remove TBA<sup>+</sup> ions, washed with DI water, and then freeze-dried. At last, HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> nanosheets were dehydrated at 400 °C for 1 h under air atmosphere to obtained Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> nanosheets. The bulk Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> was prepared by annealing of bulk HTiNbO<sub>5</sub> without chemical exfoliation.</p>
         
         <p><i>Materials Characterization</i>: The morphology of the as-prepared samples was investigated by field-emission (FE)-SEM (Zeiss Gemini DSM 982), energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX, Phoenix), and TEM (JEOL ARM 200F microscope equipped with a cold field emission electron source, and image C<sub>s</sub> corrector (CEOS GmbH)). The crystal structures of the as-prepared samples were recorded by XRD (Philips) using Cu Kα radiation. Bruker's Dimension Icon AFM System was used to record AFM images in tapping mode under ambient conditions. The XPS measurements were performed with an Kratos Axis Ultra XPS system using monochromatized Al Kalpha (1486.6 eV) radiation with a base pressure below 1 × 10<sup>−9</sup> mbar. The high resolution spectra were acquired with pass energy of 20 eV. The powder sample was pressed on an Indium foil for the measurements. The C1s peak is used as an internal standard for the calibration of the binding energy scale.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: The working electrode was prepared by mixing 70 wt% active material, 20 wt% carbon black, and 10 wt% carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) in water. The slurries was then spread uniformly on aluminum foil and dried under vacuum at 80 °C for 12 h. The cells were assembled in an argon-filled glove box using Ti<sub>2</sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>9</sub> as the working electrode and Na metal as the counter and reference electrode in coin type cells. A 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> solution in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether was used as the electrolyte and glass fibers was used as the separator. The galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were performed on a battery system (Neware BTS). CV studies were carried out on an electrochemical workstation (CH Instruments, model 604E).</p>
         
         <p><i>DFT Calculations</i>: DFT calculations were performed within the PAW method and PBE functional implemented in the Vienna Ab-initio Simulation Package (VASP) package.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0043_R_d10723946e1786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Phys. Rev. B">43</a>-<a href="#adma201804378-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0047_R_d10723946e1786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Phys. Rev. B">47</a></span> The dispersion correction is considered by adopting Grimme's D3 scheme.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0048_R_d10723946e1788" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Chem. Phys.">48</a></span> The cutoff energy is set to be 500 eV and reduced Brillouin zone is sampled with a 3 × 3 × 1 <i>k</i>-mesh.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0049_R_d10723946e1792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Phys. Rev. B">49</a></span> The force and energy convergence criteria for each atom is 0.01 and 10<sup>−5</sup>eV. The migration barrier is calculated using the climbing image nudged-elastic band (CI-NEB) method and optimized until the forces perpendicular to the path ≤0.02 eV Å<sup>−1</sup>.<span><a href="#adma201804378-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201804378-bib-0050_R_d10723946e1799" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Chem. Phys.">50</a></span></p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
